<p><b>Animals</b> are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Multicellular" title="Multicellular">multicellular</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eukaryotic" title="Eukaryotic">eukaryotic</a> organisms in the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_(biology)" title="Kingdom (biology)">biological kingdom</a> <b>Animalia</b>. With few exceptions, animals <a href="/wiki/Heterotroph" title="Heterotroph">consume organic material</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cellular_respiration#Aerobic_respiration" title="Cellular respiration">breathe oxygen</a>, are <a href="/wiki/Motility" title="Motility">able to move</a>, can <a href="/wiki/Sexual_reproduction" title="Sexual reproduction">reproduce sexually</a>, and go through an ontogenetic stage in which their body consists of a hollow sphere of <a href="/wiki/Cell_(biology)" title="Cell (biology)">cells</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blastula" title="Blastula">blastula</a>, during <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Embryogenesis" title="Embryogenesis">embryonic development</a>. Over 1.5 million <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extant_taxon" title="Extant taxon">living</a> animal <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a> have been <a href="/wiki/Species_description" title="Species description">described</a>—of which around 1 million are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Insecta" title="Insecta">insects</a>—but it has been estimated there are over 7 million animal species in total. Animals range in length from 8.5 micrometres (0.00033 in) to 33.6 metres (110 ft). They have <a href="/wiki/Ecology" title="Ecology">complex interactions</a> with each other and their environments, forming intricate <a href="/wiki/Food_web" title="Food web">food webs</a>. The scientific study of animals is known as <a href="/wiki/Zoology" title="Zoology">zoology</a>.
</p><p>Most living animal species are in <a href="/wiki/Bilateria" title="Bilateria">Bilateria</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Clade" title="Clade">clade</a> whose members have a <a href="/wiki/Symmetry_in_biology#Bilateral_symmetry" title="Symmetry in biology">bilaterally symmetric</a> <a href="/wiki/Body_plan" title="Body plan">body plan</a>. The Bilateria include the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protostomes" title="Protostomes">protostomes</a>, containing animals such as <a href="/wiki/Nematode" title="Nematode">nematodes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arthropod" title="Arthropod">arthropods</a>, <a href="/wiki/Flatworm" title="Flatworm">flatworms</a>, <a href="/wiki/Annelid" title="Annelid">annelids</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mollusc" title="Mollusc">molluscs</a>, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deuterostomes" title="Deuterostomes">deuterostomes</a>, containing the <a href="/wiki/Echinoderm" title="Echinoderm">echinoderms</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Chordate" title="Chordate">chordates</a>, the latter including the <a href="/wiki/Vertebrate" title="Vertebrate">vertebrates</a>. Life forms interpreted as early animals were present in the <a href="/wiki/Ediacaran_biota" title="Ediacaran biota">Ediacaran biota</a> of the late <a href="/wiki/Precambrian" title="Precambrian">Precambrian</a>. Many modern animal <a href="/wiki/Phylum" title="Phylum">phyla</a> became clearly established in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fossil_record" title="Fossil record">fossil record</a> as <a href="/wiki/Marine_life" title="Marine life">marine species</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Cambrian_explosion" title="Cambrian explosion">Cambrian explosion</a>, which began around 539 million years ago. 6,331 groups of <a href="/wiki/Gene" title="Gene">genes</a> common to all living animals have been identified; these may have arisen from a single <a href="#Phylogeny">common ancestor</a> that lived <a href="/wiki/Cryogenian" title="Cryogenian">650 million years ago</a>.
</p><p>Historically, <a href="/wiki/Aristotle%27s_biology" title="Aristotle's biology">Aristotle divided animals</a> into those with blood and those without. <a href="/wiki/Carl_Linnaeus" title="Carl Linnaeus">Carl Linnaeus</a> created the first hierarchical <a href="/wiki/Taxonomy_(biology)" title="Taxonomy (biology)">biological classification</a> for animals in 1758 with his <i><a href="/wiki/Systema_Naturae" title="Systema Naturae">Systema Naturae</a></i>, which <a href="/wiki/Jean-Baptiste_Lamarck" title="Jean-Baptiste Lamarck">Jean-Baptiste Lamarck</a> expanded into 14 <a href="/wiki/Phylum" title="Phylum">phyla</a> by 1809. In 1874, <a href="/wiki/Ernst_Haeckel" title="Ernst Haeckel">Ernst Haeckel</a> divided the animal kingdom into the multicellular <b>Metazoa</b> (now <a href="/wiki/Synonym_(taxonomy)" title="Synonym (taxonomy)">synonymous</a> for Animalia) and the <a href="/wiki/Protozoa" title="Protozoa">Protozoa</a>, single-celled organisms no longer considered animals. In modern times, the biological classification of animals relies on advanced techniques, such as <a href="/wiki/Molecular_phylogenetics" title="Molecular phylogenetics">molecular phylogenetics</a>, which are effective at demonstrating the <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolutionary</a> relationships between <a href="/wiki/Taxon" title="Taxon">taxa</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Human" title="Human">Humans</a> make <a href="/wiki/Human_uses_of_animals" title="Human uses of animals">use of many animal species</a>, such as for food (including <a href="/wiki/Meat" title="Meat">meat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Milk" title="Milk">milk</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Egg" title="Egg">eggs</a>), for materials (such as <a href="/wiki/Leather" title="Leather">leather</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wool" title="Wool">wool</a>), as <a href="/wiki/Pet" title="Pet">pets</a>, and as <a href="/wiki/Working_animal" title="Working animal">working animals</a> including for transport. <a href="/wiki/Dog" title="Dog">Dogs</a> have been <a href="/wiki/Hunting_dog" title="Hunting dog">used in hunting</a>, as have <a href="/wiki/Falconry" title="Falconry">birds of prey</a>, while many terrestrial and <a href="/wiki/Aquatic_animal" title="Aquatic animal">aquatic animals</a> were hunted for sports. Nonhuman animals have appeared in art from the earliest times and are featured in mythology and religion.
</p>
<div aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading" class="toc" id="toc" role="navigation"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h2>
<p>The word "animal" comes from the Latin <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/animalis#Latin" title="wikt:animalis">animalis</a></i></span>, meaning 'having breath', 'having soul' or 'living being'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> The biological definition includes all members of the kingdom Animalia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-americanheritage_animal_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-americanheritage_animal-2">[2]</a></sup> In colloquial usage, the term <i>animal</i> is often used to refer only to nonhuman animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> The term "metazoa" is from  Ancient Greek μετα (<i>meta</i>, used to mean "later") and ζῷᾰ (<i>zōia</i>, plural of ζῷον <i>zōion</i> "animal").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Characteristics">Characteristics</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Blastulation.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="344" data-file-width="799" decoding="async" height="125" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Blastulation.png/290px-Blastulation.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Blastulation.png/435px-Blastulation.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Blastulation.png/580px-Blastulation.png 2x" width="290"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Blastulation.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Animals are unique in having the ball of cells of the early <a href="/wiki/Embryo" title="Embryo">embryo</a> (1) develop into a hollow ball or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blastula" title="Blastula">blastula</a> (2).</div></div></div>
<p>Animals have several characteristics that set them apart from other living things. Animals are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eukaryotic" title="Eukaryotic">eukaryotic</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Multicellular" title="Multicellular">multicellular</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Avila1995_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Avila1995-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-palaeos_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-palaeos-10">[10]</a></sup> Unlike plants and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alga" title="Alga">algae</a>, which <a href="/wiki/Autotroph" title="Autotroph">produce their own nutrients</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AnimalCells_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-AnimalCells-11">[11]</a></sup> animals are <a href="/wiki/Heterotroph" title="Heterotroph">heterotrophic</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-palaeos_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-palaeos-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Windows_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Windows-12">[12]</a></sup> feeding on organic material and digesting it internally.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> With very few exceptions, (example; <i><a href="/wiki/Henneguya_zschokkei" title="Henneguya zschokkei">Henneguya zschokkei</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNN-20200226_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-CNN-20200226-14">[14]</a></sup>) animals <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aerobic_respiration" title="Aerobic respiration">respire aerobically</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oxygen_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-oxygen-15">[15]</a></sup> All animals are <a href="/wiki/Motility" title="Motility">motile</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Concepts_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Concepts-16">[16]</a></sup> (able to spontaneously move their bodies) during at least part of their <a href="/wiki/Biological_life_cycle" title="Biological life cycle">life cycle</a>, but some animals, such as <a href="/wiki/Sponge" title="Sponge">sponges</a>, <a href="/wiki/Coral" title="Coral">corals</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mussel" title="Mussel">mussels</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Barnacle" title="Barnacle">barnacles</a>, later become <a href="/wiki/Sessility_(motility)" title="Sessility (motility)">sessile</a>. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blastula" title="Blastula">blastula</a> is a stage in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Embryogenesis" title="Embryogenesis">embryonic development</a> that is unique to animals, allowing <a href="/wiki/Cellular_differentiation" title="Cellular differentiation">cells to be differentiated</a> into specialised tissues and organs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Structure">Structure</span></h3>
<p>All animals are composed of cells, surrounded by a characteristic <a href="/wiki/Extracellular_matrix" title="Extracellular matrix">extracellular matrix</a> composed of <a href="/wiki/Collagen" title="Collagen">collagen</a> and elastic <a href="/wiki/Glycoprotein" title="Glycoprotein">glycoproteins</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> During development, the animal extracellular matrix forms a relatively flexible framework upon which cells can move about and be reorganised, making the formation of complex structures possible. This may be calcified, forming structures such as <a href="/wiki/Exoskeleton" title="Exoskeleton">shells</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bone" title="Bone">bones</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spicule_(sponge)" title="Spicule (sponge)">spicules</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> In contrast, the cells of other multicellular organisms (primarily algae, plants, and fungi) are held in place by cell walls, and so develop by progressive growth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> Animal cells uniquely possess the <a href="/wiki/Cell_junction" title="Cell junction">cell junctions</a> called <a href="/wiki/Tight_junction" title="Tight junction">tight junctions</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gap_junction" title="Gap junction">gap junctions</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Desmosome" title="Desmosome">desmosomes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>With few exceptions—in particular, the sponges and <a href="/wiki/Placozoa" title="Placozoa">placozoans</a>—animal bodies are differentiated into <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biological_tissue" title="Biological tissue">tissues</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> These include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muscle" title="Muscle">muscles</a>, which enable locomotion, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nerve_tissue" title="Nerve tissue">nerve tissues</a>, which transmit signals and coordinate the body. Typically, there is also an internal <a href="/wiki/Digestion" title="Digestion">digestive</a> chamber with either one opening (in Ctenophora, Cnidaria, and flatworms) or two openings (in most bilaterians).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reproduction_and_development">Reproduction and development</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Odonata_copulation.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1359" data-file-width="2117" decoding="async" height="141" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Odonata_copulation.jpg/220px-Odonata_copulation.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Odonata_copulation.jpg/330px-Odonata_copulation.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Odonata_copulation.jpg/440px-Odonata_copulation.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Odonata_copulation.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Sexual_reproduction" title="Sexual reproduction">Sexual reproduction</a> is nearly universal in animals, such as these <a href="/wiki/Dragonfly" title="Dragonfly">dragonflies</a>.</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Sexual_reproduction#Animals" title="Sexual reproduction">Sexual reproduction § Animals</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Asexual_reproduction#Examples_in_animals" title="Asexual reproduction">Asexual reproduction § Examples in animals</a></div>
<p>Nearly all animals make use of some form of sexual reproduction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> They produce <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Haploid" title="Haploid">haploid</a> <a href="/wiki/Gamete" title="Gamete">gametes</a> by <a href="/wiki/Meiosis" title="Meiosis">meiosis</a>; the smaller, motile gametes are <a href="/wiki/Spermatozoon" title="Spermatozoon">spermatozoa</a> and the larger, non-motile gametes are <a href="/wiki/Egg_cell" title="Egg cell">ova</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> These fuse to form <a href="/wiki/Zygote" title="Zygote">zygotes</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> which develop via <a href="/wiki/Mitosis" title="Mitosis">mitosis</a> into a hollow sphere, called a blastula. In sponges, blastula larvae swim to a new location, attach to the seabed, and develop into a new sponge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> In most other groups, the blastula  undergoes more complicated rearrangement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> It first <a href="/wiki/Invagination" title="Invagination">invaginates</a> to form a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gastrula" title="Gastrula">gastrula</a> with a digestive chamber and two separate <a href="/wiki/Germ_layer" title="Germ layer">germ layers</a>, an external <a href="/wiki/Ectoderm" title="Ectoderm">ectoderm</a> and an internal <a href="/wiki/Endoderm" title="Endoderm">endoderm</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> In most cases, a third germ layer, the <a href="/wiki/Mesoderm" title="Mesoderm">mesoderm</a>, also develops between them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> These germ layers then differentiate to form tissues and organs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p><p>Repeated instances of <a href="/wiki/Inbreeding" title="Inbreeding">mating with a close relative</a> during sexual reproduction generally leads to <a href="/wiki/Inbreeding_depression" title="Inbreeding depression">inbreeding depression</a> within a population due to the increased prevalence of harmful <a href="/wiki/Dominance_(genetics)" title="Dominance (genetics)">recessive</a> traits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid19834483_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid19834483-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid3324702_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid3324702-33">[33]</a></sup> Animals have evolved numerous mechanisms for <a href="/wiki/Inbreeding_avoidance" title="Inbreeding avoidance">avoiding close inbreeding</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pusey_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pusey-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>
Some animals are capable of <a href="/wiki/Asexual_reproduction" title="Asexual reproduction">asexual reproduction</a>, which often results in a genetic clone of the parent. This may take place through <a href="/wiki/Fragmentation_(reproduction)" title="Fragmentation (reproduction)">fragmentation</a>; <a href="/wiki/Budding" title="Budding">budding</a>, such as in <a href="/wiki/Hydra_(genus)" title="Hydra (genus)"><i>Hydra</i></a> and other <a href="/wiki/Cnidaria" title="Cnidaria">cnidarians</a>; or <a href="/wiki/Parthenogenesis" title="Parthenogenesis">parthenogenesis</a>, where fertile eggs are produced without <a href="/wiki/Mating" title="Mating">mating</a>, such as in <a href="/wiki/Aphid" title="Aphid">aphids</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup></p><div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ecology">Ecology</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ultramarine_Flycatcher_(Ficedula_superciliaris)_Naggar,_Himachal_Pradesh,_2013_(cropped).JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1781" data-file-width="1383" decoding="async" height="219" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Ultramarine_Flycatcher_%28Ficedula_superciliaris%29_Naggar%2C_Himachal_Pradesh%2C_2013_%28cropped%29.JPG/170px-Ultramarine_Flycatcher_%28Ficedula_superciliaris%29_Naggar%2C_Himachal_Pradesh%2C_2013_%28cropped%29.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Ultramarine_Flycatcher_%28Ficedula_superciliaris%29_Naggar%2C_Himachal_Pradesh%2C_2013_%28cropped%29.JPG/255px-Ultramarine_Flycatcher_%28Ficedula_superciliaris%29_Naggar%2C_Himachal_Pradesh%2C_2013_%28cropped%29.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Ultramarine_Flycatcher_%28Ficedula_superciliaris%29_Naggar%2C_Himachal_Pradesh%2C_2013_%28cropped%29.JPG/340px-Ultramarine_Flycatcher_%28Ficedula_superciliaris%29_Naggar%2C_Himachal_Pradesh%2C_2013_%28cropped%29.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ultramarine_Flycatcher_(Ficedula_superciliaris)_Naggar,_Himachal_Pradesh,_2013_(cropped).JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Predator" title="Predator">Predators</a>, such as this <a href="/wiki/Ultramarine_flycatcher" title="Ultramarine flycatcher">ultramarine flycatcher</a> (<i>Ficedula superciliaris</i>), feed on other animals.</div></div></div>
<p>Animals are categorised into <a href="/wiki/Ecology" title="Ecology">ecological</a> groups depending on how they obtain or consume organic material, including <a href="/wiki/Carnivore" title="Carnivore">carnivores</a>, <a href="/wiki/Herbivore" title="Herbivore">herbivores</a>, <a href="/wiki/Omnivore" title="Omnivore">omnivores</a>, <a href="/wiki/Detritivore" title="Detritivore">detritivores</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parasite" title="Parasite">parasites</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> Interactions between animals form complex <a href="/wiki/Food_web" title="Food web">food webs</a>. In carnivorous or omnivorous species, <a href="/wiki/Predation" title="Predation">predation</a> is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Consumer-resource_systems" title="Consumer-resource systems">consumer–resource interaction</a> where a predator feeds on another organism (called its <i>prey</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ecology_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ecology-39">[39]</a></sup> Selective pressures imposed on one another lead to an <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_arms_race" title="Evolutionary arms race">evolutionary arms race</a> between predator and prey, resulting in various <a href="/wiki/Anti-predator_adaptation" title="Anti-predator adaptation">anti-predator adaptations</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> Almost all multicellular predators are animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SimpsonCB_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-SimpsonCB-42">[42]</a></sup> Some <a href="/wiki/Consumer_(food_chain)" title="Consumer (food chain)">consumers</a> use multiple methods; for example, in <a href="/wiki/Parasitoid_wasp" title="Parasitoid wasp">parasitoid wasps</a>, the larvae feed on the hosts' living tissues, killing them in the process,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> but the adults primarily consume nectar from flowers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> Other animals may have very specific <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Feeding_behaviour" title="Feeding behaviour">feeding behaviours</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Hawksbill_sea_turtle" title="Hawksbill sea turtle">hawksbill sea turtles</a> primarily <a href="/wiki/Spongivore" title="Spongivore">eating sponges</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ScienceSpongi_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-ScienceSpongi-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Expl0072_-_Flickr_-_NOAA_Photo_Library.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1920" data-file-width="2560" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Expl0072_-_Flickr_-_NOAA_Photo_Library.jpg/220px-Expl0072_-_Flickr_-_NOAA_Photo_Library.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Expl0072_-_Flickr_-_NOAA_Photo_Library.jpg/330px-Expl0072_-_Flickr_-_NOAA_Photo_Library.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Expl0072_-_Flickr_-_NOAA_Photo_Library.jpg/440px-Expl0072_-_Flickr_-_NOAA_Photo_Library.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Expl0072_-_Flickr_-_NOAA_Photo_Library.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Hydrothermal_vent" title="Hydrothermal vent">Hydrothermal vent</a> mussels and shrimps</div></div></div>
<p>Most animals rely on the biomass and energy produced by plants through <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthesis</a>. Herbivores eat plant material directly, while carnivores, and other animals on higher <a href="/wiki/Trophic_level" title="Trophic level">trophic levels</a> typically acquire it indirectly by eating other animals. Animals oxidize <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate" title="Carbohydrate">carbohydrates</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lipid" title="Lipid">lipids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">proteins</a>, and other biomolecules, which allows the animal to grow and to sustain biological processes such as <a href="/wiki/Animal_locomotion" title="Animal locomotion">locomotion</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> Animals living close to <a href="/wiki/Hydrothermal_vent" title="Hydrothermal vent">hydrothermal vents</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cold_seep" title="Cold seep">cold seeps</a> on the dark <a href="/wiki/Seabed" title="Seabed">sea floor</a> consume organic matter of <a href="/wiki/Archaea" title="Archaea">archaea</a> and bacteria produced in these locations through <a href="/wiki/Chemosynthesis" title="Chemosynthesis">chemosynthesis</a> (by oxidizing inorganic compounds, such as <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_sulfide" title="Hydrogen sulfide">hydrogen sulfide</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p><p>Animals originally evolved in the sea. Lineages of arthropods colonised land around the same time as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Land_plant" title="Land plant">land plants</a>, probably between 510 and 471 million years ago during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Late_Cambrian" title="Late Cambrian">Late Cambrian</a> or Early <a href="/wiki/Ordovician" title="Ordovician">Ordovician</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rota-Stabelli2013_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rota-Stabelli2013-50">[50]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Vertebrate" title="Vertebrate">Vertebrates</a> such as the <a href="/wiki/Sarcopterygii" title="Sarcopterygii">lobe-finned fish</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Tiktaalik" title="Tiktaalik">Tiktaalik</a></i> started to move on to land in the late <a href="/wiki/Devonian" title="Devonian">Devonian</a>, about 375 million years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> Animals occupy virtually all of earth's <a href="/wiki/Habitat" title="Habitat">habitats</a> and microhabitats, including salt water, hydrothermal vents, fresh water, hot springs, swamps, forests, pastures, deserts, air, and the interiors of animals, plants, fungi and rocks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Margulis_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Margulis-53">[53]</a></sup> Animals are however not particularly <a href="/wiki/Thermophile" title="Thermophile">heat tolerant</a>; very few of them can survive at constant temperatures above 50 °C (122 °F).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clarke2014_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Clarke2014-54">[54]</a></sup> Only very few species of animals (mostly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nematodes" title="Nematodes">nematodes</a>) inhabit the most extreme cold deserts of continental <a href="/wiki/Antarctica" title="Antarctica">Antarctica</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bas-land_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-bas-land-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Diversity">Diversity</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Anim1754_-_Flickr_-_NOAA_Photo_Library_(1).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1950" data-file-width="2600" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Anim1754_-_Flickr_-_NOAA_Photo_Library_%281%29.jpg/220px-Anim1754_-_Flickr_-_NOAA_Photo_Library_%281%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Anim1754_-_Flickr_-_NOAA_Photo_Library_%281%29.jpg/330px-Anim1754_-_Flickr_-_NOAA_Photo_Library_%281%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Anim1754_-_Flickr_-_NOAA_Photo_Library_%281%29.jpg/440px-Anim1754_-_Flickr_-_NOAA_Photo_Library_%281%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Anim1754_-_Flickr_-_NOAA_Photo_Library_(1).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Blue_whale" title="Blue whale">blue whale</a> is the largest animal that has ever lived.</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Size">Size</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Largest_organisms" title="Largest organisms">Largest organisms</a> and <a href="/wiki/Smallest_organisms" title="Smallest organisms">Smallest organisms</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Blue_whale" title="Blue whale">blue whale</a> (<i>Balaenoptera musculus</i>) is the largest animal that has ever lived, weighing up to 190 <a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">tonnes</a> and measuring up to 33.6 metres (110 ft) long.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wood_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wood-56">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> The largest extant terrestrial animal is the <a href="/wiki/African_bush_elephant" title="African bush elephant">African bush elephant</a> (<i>Loxodonta africana</i>), weighing up to 12.25 tonnes<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wood_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wood-56">[56]</a></sup> and measuring up to 10.67 metres (35.0 ft) long.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wood_56-2"><a href="#cite_note-Wood-56">[56]</a></sup> The largest terrestrial animals that ever lived were <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Titanosaur" title="Titanosaur">titanosaur</a> <a href="/wiki/Sauropoda" title="Sauropoda">sauropod dinosaurs</a> such as <i><a href="/wiki/Argentinosaurus" title="Argentinosaurus">Argentinosaurus</a></i>, which may have weighed as much as 73 tonnes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mazzettaetal2004_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mazzettaetal2004-59">[59]</a></sup> Several animals are microscopic; some <a href="/wiki/Myxozoa" title="Myxozoa">Myxozoa</a> (<a href="/wiki/Obligate_parasite" title="Obligate parasite">obligate parasites</a> within the Cnidaria) never grow larger than 20 <a href="/wiki/Micrometre" title="Micrometre">µm</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> and one of the smallest species (<i>Myxobolus shekel</i>) is no more than 8.5 µm when fully grown.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Numbers_and_habitats">Numbers and habitats</span></h3>
<p>The following table lists estimated numbers of described extant species for the animal groups with the largest numbers of species,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zhang2013_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zhang2013-62">[62]</a></sup> along with their principal habitats (terrestrial, fresh water,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Balian2008_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Balian2008-63">[63]</a></sup> and marine),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hogenboom2016_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hogenboom2016-64">[64]</a></sup> and free-living or parasitic ways of life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Poulin2007_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Poulin2007-65">[65]</a></sup> Species estimates shown here are based on numbers described scientifically; much larger estimates have been calculated based on various means of prediction, and these can vary wildly. For instance, around 25,000–27,000 species of nematodes have been described, while published estimates of the total number of nematode species include 10,000–20,000; 500,000; 10 million; and 100 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Felder2009_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-Felder2009-66">[66]</a></sup> Using patterns within the <a href="/wiki/Taxonomy_(biology)" title="Taxonomy (biology)">taxonomic</a> hierarchy, the total number of animal species—including those not yet described—was calculated to be about 7.77 million in 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mora2011_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mora2011-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[a]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Evolutionary_origin">Evolutionary origin</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Urmetazoan" title="Urmetazoan">Urmetazoan</a></div>
<p>Animals are found as long ago as the <a href="/wiki/Ediacaran_biota" title="Ediacaran biota">Ediacaran biota</a>, towards the end of the Precambrian, and possibly somewhat earlier. It had long been doubted whether these life-forms included animals,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[82]</a></sup> but the discovery of the animal lipid <a href="/wiki/Cholesterol" title="Cholesterol">cholesterol</a> in fossils of <i><a href="/wiki/Dickinsonia" title="Dickinsonia">Dickinsonia</a></i> establishes their nature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bobrovskiy_Hope_Ivantsov_Nettersheim_pp._1246–1249_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bobrovskiy_Hope_Ivantsov_Nettersheim_pp._1246–1249-85">[83]</a></sup> Animals are thought to have originated under low-oxygen conditions, suggesting that they were capable of living entirely by <a href="/wiki/Anaerobic_respiration" title="Anaerobic respiration">anaerobic respiration</a>, but as they became specialized for aerobic metabolism they became fully dependent on oxygen in their environments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zimorski2019_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zimorski2019-86">[84]</a></sup>
</p><p>Many animal phyla first appear in the <a href="/wiki/Fossil" title="Fossil">fossil</a> record during the <a href="/wiki/Cambrian_explosion" title="Cambrian explosion">Cambrian explosion</a>, starting about 539 million years ago, in beds such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Burgess_shale" title="Burgess shale">Burgess shale</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[85]</a></sup> Extant phyla in these rocks include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mollusc" title="Mollusc">molluscs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brachiopod" title="Brachiopod">brachiopods</a>, <a href="/wiki/Onychophora" title="Onychophora">onychophorans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tardigrade" title="Tardigrade">tardigrades</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arthropod" title="Arthropod">arthropods</a>, <a href="/wiki/Echinoderm" title="Echinoderm">echinoderms</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hemichordate" title="Hemichordate">hemichordates</a>, along with numerous now-extinct forms such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Predator" title="Predator">predatory</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Anomalocaris" title="Anomalocaris">Anomalocaris</a></i>. The apparent suddenness of the event may however be an artefact of the fossil record, rather than showing that all these animals appeared simultaneously.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Royal_Ontario_Museum_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-Royal_Ontario_Museum-91">[89]</a></sup> 
That view is supported by the discovery of <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Auroralumina_attenboroughii" title="Auroralumina attenboroughii">Auroralumina attenboroughii</a></i>, the earliest known Ediacaran crown-group cnidarian (557–562 mya, some 20 million years before the Cambrian explosion) from <a href="/wiki/Charnwood_Forest" title="Charnwood Forest">Charnwood Forest</a>, England. It is thought to be one of the earliest <a href="/wiki/Predation" title="Predation">predators</a>, catching small prey with its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nematocyst" title="Nematocyst">nematocysts</a> as modern cnidarians do.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunn_Kenchington_Parry_Clark_2022_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dunn_Kenchington_Parry_Clark_2022-92">[90]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some palaeontologists have suggested that animals appeared much earlier than the Cambrian explosion, possibly as early as 1 billion years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[91]</a></sup> Early fossils that might represent animals appear for example in the 665-million-year-old rocks of the <a href="/wiki/Trezona_Formation" title="Trezona Formation">Trezona Formation</a> of <a href="/wiki/South_Australia" title="South Australia">South Australia</a>. These fossils are interpreted as most probably being early <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Porifera" title="Porifera">sponges</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-roseMaloof_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-roseMaloof-94">[92]</a></sup>
<a href="/wiki/Trace_fossil" title="Trace fossil">Trace fossils</a> such as tracks and burrows found in the <a href="/wiki/Tonian" title="Tonian">Tonian</a> period (from 1 gya) may indicate the presence of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Triploblastic" title="Triploblastic">triploblastic</a> worm-like animals, roughly as large (about 5 mm wide) and complex as earthworms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Seilacher1998_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-Seilacher1998-95">[93]</a></sup> However, similar tracks are produced today by the giant single-celled protist <i><a href="/wiki/Gromia_sphaerica" title="Gromia sphaerica">Gromia sphaerica</a></i>, so the Tonian trace fossils may not indicate early animal evolution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Matz2008_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-Matz2008-96">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MSNBC200811_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-MSNBC200811-97">[95]</a></sup> Around the same time, the layered mats of <a href="/wiki/Microorganism" title="Microorganism">microorganisms</a> called <a href="/wiki/Stromatolite" title="Stromatolite">stromatolites</a> decreased in diversity, perhaps due to grazing by newly evolved animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bengtson2002OriginsOfPredation_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bengtson2002OriginsOfPredation-98">[96]</a></sup> Objects such as sediment-filled tubes that resemble trace fossils of the burrows of wormlike animals have been found in 1.2 gya rocks in North America, in 1.5 gya rocks in Australia and North America, and in 1.7 gya rocks in Australia. Their interpretation as having an animal origin is disputed, as they might be water-escape or other structures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Seilacher_20072_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-Seilacher_20072-99">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[98]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 248.66666666667px"><div style="width: 248.66666666667px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 246.66666666667px;"><div style="margin:-0.16666666666667px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:DickinsoniaCostata.jpg" title="Dickinsonia costata from the Ediacaran biota (c. 635–542 mya) is one of the earliest animal species known.[83]"><img alt="Dickinsonia costata from the Ediacaran biota (c. 635–542 mya) is one of the earliest animal species known.[83]" data-file-height="1704" data-file-width="2272" decoding="async" height="186" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/DickinsoniaCostata.jpg/370px-DickinsoniaCostata.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/DickinsoniaCostata.jpg/555px-DickinsoniaCostata.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/DickinsoniaCostata.jpg/740px-DickinsoniaCostata.jpg 2x" width="247"/></a></div></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Phylogeny">Phylogeny</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Lists_of_animals" title="Lists of animals">Lists of animals</a></div>
<p>Animals are <a href="/wiki/Monophyly" title="Monophyly">monophyletic</a>, meaning they are derived from a common ancestor. Animals are sister to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Choanoflagellata" title="Choanoflagellata">Choanoflagellata</a>, with which they form the <a href="/wiki/Choanozoa" title="Choanozoa">Choanozoa</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Budd2015_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-Budd2015-101">[99]</a></sup> The most <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Basal_animal" title="Basal animal">basal animals</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Porifera" title="Porifera">Porifera</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ctenophora" title="Ctenophora">Ctenophora</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cnidaria" title="Cnidaria">Cnidaria</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Placozoa" title="Placozoa">Placozoa</a>, have body plans that lack <a href="/wiki/Symmetry_in_biology" title="Symmetry in biology">bilateral symmetry</a>. Their relationships are still disputed; the sister group to all other animals could be the Porifera or the Ctenophora,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kapli_2020_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kapli_2020-102">[100]</a></sup> both of which lack <a href="/wiki/Hox_gene" title="Hox gene">hox genes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_developmental_biology#Gene_toolkit" title="Evolutionary developmental biology">important in body plan development</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Giribet_2016_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-Giribet_2016-103">[101]</a></sup>
</p><p>These genes are found in the Placozoa<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dellaporta2004_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dellaporta2004-105">[103]</a></sup> and the higher animals, the Bilateria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peterson2001_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-Peterson2001-106">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KraemerEis2016_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-KraemerEis2016-107">[105]</a></sup> 6,331 groups of <a href="/wiki/Gene" title="Gene">genes</a> common to all living animals have been identified; these may have arisen from a single <a href="#Phylogeny">common ancestor</a> that lived <a href="/wiki/Cryogenian" title="Cryogenian">650 million years ago</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Precambrian" title="Precambrian">Precambrian</a>. 25 of these are novel core gene groups, found only in animals; of those, 8 are for essential components of the <a href="/wiki/Wnt_signaling_pathway" title="Wnt signaling pathway">Wnt</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/TGF-beta" title="TGF-beta">TGF-beta</a> signalling pathways which may have enabled animals to become multicellular by providing a pattern for the body's system of axes (in three dimensions), and another 7 are for <a href="/wiki/Transcription_factor" title="Transcription factor">transcription factors</a> including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homeodomain" title="Homeodomain">homeodomain</a> proteins involved in the <a href="/wiki/Evo-devo_gene_toolkit" title="Evo-devo gene toolkit">control of development</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zimmer2018_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zimmer2018-108">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Paps2018_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-Paps2018-109">[107]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Phylogenetic_tree" title="Phylogenetic tree">phylogenetic tree</a> (of major lineages only) indicates approximately how many millions of years ago (<em>mya</em>) the lineages split.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[111]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[112]</a></sup>
</p>

</td>
<td class="clade-leaf" rowspan="2">
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Platyhelminthes" title="Platyhelminthes">Platyhelminthes</a> and allies <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sorocelis_reticulosa.jpg"><img alt="Sorocelis reticulosa.jpg" data-file-height="208" data-file-width="619" decoding="async" height="20" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Sorocelis_reticulosa.jpg/60px-Sorocelis_reticulosa.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Sorocelis_reticulosa.jpg/90px-Sorocelis_reticulosa.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Sorocelis_reticulosa.jpg/120px-Sorocelis_reticulosa.jpg 2x" width="60"/></a>
